maassa.</span><span> <!-- -->Image: Jyrki LyytikkÃ¤ / Yle</span></figcaption></figure><div class="sc-dtckWT eYygKv"><div class="sc-ewMzwg fjutvz yle__article__date"><time class="sc-bCCZMN kVdjPA yle__article__date--published" dateTime="2016-01-13T13:21:26+02:00">13.1.2016 13:21</time><span aria-hidden="true" class="sc-ggPeai fOTnuz">â€¢</span><time class="sc-bCCZMN kVdjPA yle__article__date--modified" dateTime="2016-01-14T07:58:44+02:00">Updated<!-- --> <!-- -->14.1.2016 7:58</time></div></div></header><section class="yle__article__content"><p class="sc-cYoYeT sc-gkCgsS bGefQh boudIS yle__article__paragraph">In 2013 Finlandâ€™s Foreign Ministry had its systems hacked by what investigators described as â€˜a state actorâ€™. Now Yle&#x27;s sources have confirmed who was behind the attack: the Turla group of Russian-speaking hackers who perpetrated attacks on targets in more than 50 countries worldwide during the same period.</p><p class="sc-cYoYeT sc-gkCgsS bGefQh boudIS yle__article__paragraph">Stefan Tanase of Kaspersky security says that the Turla group is the premier Russian hacker organizationâ€”and it targets ministries, embassies and militaries in Russiaâ€™s neighbours.Â </p><p class="sc-cYoYeT sc-gkCgsS bGefQh boudIS yle__article__paragraph">&quot;We believe that the Turla group is a nation state-sponsored attacker,&quot; said Tanase. &quot;We have seen traces in their malware and their servers, which we analyse, that point to the fact that the authors are Russian speaking and they definitely seem to have lot of resources to their cyber-espionage operation.&quot;</p><p class="sc-cYoYeT sc-gkCgsS bGefQh boudIS yle__article__paragraph">Yle has confirmed from several European sources that the 2013 attacks on the Foreign Ministry were perpetrated by the Turla group. The Foreign Ministry says that it has investigated the attacks in the light of similar actions targeting other nation states around Russia.</p><p class="sc-cYoYeT sc-gkCgsS bGefQh boudIS yle__article__paragraph">&quot;The typical signs in these attack tools can be observed once you get to the stage where you know what youâ€™re looking for and can check to see if thereâ€™s anything like that, and if there have been any changes in their tools,&quot; said Ari Uusikartano of the Foreign Ministry.</p><p class="sc-cYoYeT sc-gkCgsS bGefQh boudIS yle__article__paragraph">Uusikartano says that the attack by Turla hit most countries in western Europe. Kaspersky, which published a report on the methods and targets of Turla (which did not at the time mention Finland) last autumn, has outlined the methods used by the attackers.</p><p class="sc-cYoYeT sc-gkCgsS bGefQh boudIS yle__article__paragraph">First, cyber spies start by gathering information about the target via sources like social media, public websites and internal phone directories.</p><p class="sc-cYoYeT sc-gkCgsS bGefQh boudIS yle__article__paragraph">Second, they choose a few employees to whom they send emails which include a link to an apparently interesting website, which appears to be connected somehow to the targets everyday life or area of specialization. The hackers have however embedded malware in the site concerned.</p><p class="sc-cYoYeT sc-gkCgsS bGefQh boudIS yle__article__paragraph">Third, when the employee visits the site, the malware ends up on his or her computer.</p><p class="sc-cYoYeT sc-gkCgsS bGefQh boudIS yle__article__paragraph">That allows in the attacker, as happened to the Finnish Foreign Ministry in 2013. The ministry says it&#x27;s learned it&#x27;s lessons from this attack--but the hackers may already have moved on to a new method.</p></section><footer class="yle__article__footer"><div
